Refuting Ibn Sina's Distinction: Two Arguments
The author refutes Ibn Sina's distinction with two arguments, using estimation and natural order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes Ibn Sina's distinction by showing that both positional and natural orders require finiteness, and how the case of souls demonstrates the impossibility of infinite simultaneous existence.

Core Idea
Both positional and natural order lead to impossibility of infinite regress; souls make it explicit.

In this passage, the author directly responds to Ibn Sina's attempted distinction between motions (successive) and bodies (simultaneous). He presents two independent arguments: the first uses the same estimation technique Ibn Sina used but applied to motions, showing the same contradiction arises; the second argues that even without positional order, the natural cause-effect order must be finite, and the case of souls—which are simultaneous—shows that an infinite number of them cannot exist. This lesson walks through both arguments and their logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the two distinct parts: the first starts with 'الوجه الأول' and the second with 'الوجه الثاني'.
The second part is cut off but the main point is clear.
2
Analyze the first argument
The author argues that even for motions, we can estimate them as simultaneous on an imaginary line, leading to the same contradiction of the less equaling the more. Look at the example of past and future days.
This shows that Ibn Sina's distinction is not valid because the same reasoning applies.
3
Trace the second argument
The second argument appeals to natural order (cause-effect, parent-child). This order must terminate in a first cause; otherwise infinite regress. Then the author brings up souls: if individuals are infinite, souls are simultaneous and infinite, which is impossible.
Even though the passage ends, the argument is that souls would be infinite and simultaneous, a clear contradiction.
4
Understand the refutation's force
Ibn Sina tried to avoid the problem by claiming motions are successive so infinite is possible, but the first argument shows even motions can be estimated as simultaneous. The second argument circles back: if you accept natural order, you must accept a first cause, and souls cannot be infinite.
5
Consider the broader implication
This refutation defends the classical mutakallimun position that an actual infinite is impossible, whether in bodies or motions or souls.
Think about how this connects to the existence of a necessary being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل له : جوابك عن هذا الفرق من وجهين : أحدهما : أنك فرضت النقطة في الجسم والامتداد إلى ما لا يتناهى بالوهم، وقدرت التطبيق أيضًا بالوهم، فقدر وهما أن الحركات الماضية كأنها موجودة متعاقبة، والأزمنة أيضا كأنها موجودة وهما متعاقبة على مثال خط موهوم لا يتناهى تركب من نقط متتالية والحدود في الحركات والأوقات في الأزمنة كالنقط في الخطوط والتعاقب كالتالي، والعلة التي لأجلها امتنع اللاتتناهي ووجب أن تتناهى فهو أنه مود إلى أنه يكون الأقل مثل الأكثر ، والناقص مثل الزايد، وهذا موجود في الموضعين جميعًا، ولذلك قال المتكلم : إنا إذا فرضنا الكلام في اليوم الذي نحن فيه، وقلنا ما مضى من الأيام غير متناه عندكم، وما بقي أيضا غير متناه، والباقي والماضي متساويان متماثلان في عدم التناهي، فلو فصلنا من الماضي وأضفناها إلى الباقي صار الماضي ناقصاً والباقي زايدًا، وهما متماثلان في نفي التناهي ، أدى ذلك إلى أن يكون الزايد مثل الناقص. الوجه الثاني : أنه لو لم يتحقق في الحركات والأشخاص ترتيب وضعي فقد تحقق فيها ترتيب طبيعي كالعلل والمعلولات فإنها لا بد أن تكون متناهية بالاتفاق، وذلك أن كل معلول فهو ممكن الوجود بذاته، وإنما يجب وجوده بعلته، فيتوقف وجوده على وجود علته، والكلام في علته كذلك حتى يتناهى إلى علة أولى ليست ممكنة لذاتها، كذلك نسبة كل والد إلى مولود نسبة العلة إلى معلولها، فيتوقف وجود الولد على وجود الوالد، وكذلك القول في الوالد، فهلا قلت : ينتهي إلى والد أول ومع ذلك فالأشخاص عندكم لا تتناهى، ثم ليعدد لكل شخص إنساني قد وجد نفس ناطقة وهي باقية مجتمعة في الوجود، فالأشخاص إذا كانت لا تتناهى وجاز وجودها لأنها متعاقبة لا مجتمعة معا في الوجود، فما قولك في النفوس فإنها مجتمعة وهي لا تتناهى؟ فإن قال : ليس لها ترتيب طبيعي ولا وضعي قيل : إذا ترتبت الأشخاص والدا ومولودا ترتبت
It was said to him: the answer to this distinction is from two aspects: One: you assumed the point in the body and extension ad infinitum by estimation, and you also estimated the application by estimation. Then they estimated that past motions are as if they exist successively, and times also as if they exist successively in the manner of an imaginary infinite line composed of successive points, and the boundaries in motions and times are like points in lines, and succession is like sequence. The reason why infinity is impossible and must be finite is that it leads to the less being like the more, and the deficient like the excessive. This exists in both cases. Therefore the mutakallim said: if we suppose the discussion on the day we are in, and say: past days are infinite according to you, and what remains is also infinite, and the remaining and the past are equal and similar in lack of infinitude, so if we separate from the past and add it to the remaining, the past becomes deficient and the remaining becomes excessive, while they are similar in negation of infinitude, that leads to the excessive being like the deficient. The second aspect: even if in motions and individuals there is no positional order, there is a natural order, like causes and effects, for they must be finite by agreement. Because every effect is possible in essence, and its existence is necessary only by its cause, so its existence depends on the existence of its cause, and the discussion about its cause likewise until it terminates at a first cause that is not possible in essence. Similarly, the relation of every parent to offspring is like the relation of cause to effect, so the existence of the child depends on the existence of the parent, and likewise for the parent. So why don't you say: it terminates at a first parent, yet individuals according to you are infinite? Then for every human individual there exists a rational soul which is permanent and simultaneous in existence. So if individuals are infinite and their existence is possible because they are successive not simultaneous in existence, what do you say about souls? They are simultaneous and are infinite? If he says: they have no natural or positional order, it is said: if individuals are arranged as parent and offspring, they are arranged...
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage contains the reply to Ibn Sina's distinction, consisting of two aspects. The first aspect argues that even for motions, we can imagine them as if they were simultaneous on an imaginary line, and then apply the same estimation (وهم) that leads to the contradiction of the less equaling the more. The author gives the example of past and future days: if both are infinite, moving a finite part from one to the other makes the deficient and excessive equal, which is absurd. This shows the same reasoning applies whether or not there is actual simultaneity. The second aspect argues that even if there is no positional order, there is a natural order (ترتيب طبيعي) like cause-effect and parent-child. This order must terminate in a first cause or first parent; otherwise, an infinite regress of causes would arise. The author then raises the problem of souls: if human individuals are infinite and each has a rational soul that coexists (since souls are permanent), then an infinite number of souls exist simultaneously, which is impossible because they would have no order. The passage ends mid-argument, but the point is clear: an infinite simultaneous multitude is impossible.

Key Terms
ترتيب طبيعي (natural order)
علة أولى (first cause)
نفس ناطقة (rational soul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second argument only applies to physical causes, not to souls.
Correction: The author explicitly extends it to souls, arguing that if individuals are infinite, souls (which coexist) would be infinite, leading to impossibility.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the second refutation (الوجه الثاني) against Ibn Sina?
A) Motions have a natural order.
B) There is a natural order like cause-effect that must be finite, and souls would be infinite and simultaneous, which is impossible.
C) Souls are not simultaneous.
D) The proof only works for bodies.

Key Takeaway
The refutation shows that both successive motions and simultaneous entities must be finite, and the case of souls demonstrates the impossibility of an infinite multitude in simultaneous existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first argument shows that if past days were infinite, moving a finite portion would make the deficient equal to the excessive. When you consider your own finite lifespan, do you truly believe that time is a created entity with a beginning, or do you find yourself assuming it stretches back forever? How does the contradiction in the argument push you to affirm a Creator who brought time into existence?
?
The second argument ties your existence to a causal chain ending in a first cause. Do you live as if your life is independent, or do you acknowledge at every moment that you are constantly in need of God? What would change in your daily routine if you genuinely felt that your very existence is maintained moment by moment by the First Cause?
?
If souls were infinite and simultaneous, they would be impossible. Your soul is unique and dependent on God. Have you ever felt that your soul could be self-sufficient? How does the impossibility of infinite souls reinforce the need for a Creator who gives each soul existence and mortality?

Proof Against Actual Infinity
Author presents a proof that applying infinite numbers leads to contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how applying infinite numbers leads to contradiction and why the author considers this proof decisive.

Core Idea
Actual infinite sets cannot exist because comparing them leads to absurdity.

In this passage, the author presents a powerful argument against the actual existence of infinite quantities. By considering two infinite series, human souls and human individuals, he shows that their comparison yields contradictory results. Let’s examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
The author sets up two infinite series: souls (existing together) and individuals (successive). Both are claimed to be infinite and susceptible to increase and decrease.
2
Follow the application argument
First, if you apply all individuals to all souls, they must be equal because both are infinite. Then if you decrease the individuals and apply the remainder, the result is either both still infinite (leading to greater equals lesser) or one becomes finite (contradicting infinity).
This is the core of the reductio ad absurdum.
3
Appreciate the conclusion
The author declares the argument decisive and unanswerable. Notice the extension to lines, numbers, and motion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الحكم يجري في كل عدد موجود معًا كالنفوس الإنسانية، أو متعاقبة كالأشخاص الإنسانية وعندهم النفوس غير متناهية عددا وهي قابلة للزيادة والنقصان والأشخاص الإنسانية غير متناهية عددًا وهي أيضا قابلة للزيادة والنقصان فإذا طبقت الأشخاص على النفوس تساوت لا محالة، وإن نقصت الأشخاص عددًا وطبقت الباقي على عدد النفوس فإن كان كل واحد منهما لا يتناهى كان الزائد مثل الناقص، وإن كان يتناهى فقد حصل المقصود، وتطبيق الخط على الخط كتطبيق العدد على العدد وتطبيق النفس على الشخص، والحركات والمتحركات إذا تناهت ذاتا وزمانًا ومكانا وعددًا، وهذا قاطع لا جواب عنه ولا سؤال عليه.
This judgment applies to every number existing together, like human souls, or successive, like human individuals. According to them, souls are infinite in number and are susceptible to increase and decrease, and human individuals are infinite in number and are also susceptible to increase and decrease. So if the individuals are applied to the souls, they equate necessarily. And if the individuals are decreased in number and the remainder is applied to the number of souls, then if each of them is not finite, the greater would equal the lesser; and if it is finite, then the purpose is achieved. And applying line to line is like applying number to number, and applying soul to individual, and motions and moving things if they are finite in essence, time, place and number—this is decisive, with no answer to it and no question about it.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage presents a proof against the actual existence of an infinite number. The author argues that if two infinite sets are compared (e.g., human souls and human individuals), the result is contradictory. When one set is applied to the other, they must be equal. But if one set is decreased and the remainder applied, either both remain infinite (leading to the absurdity that the greater equals the lesser) or one becomes finite (contradicting the original assumption). The same reasoning applies to lines, numbers, souls, and bodies in motion. The author concludes that this argument is decisive and unanswerable.

Key Terms
عدد غير متناهٍ
تطبيق
النفوس الإنسانية
الأشخاص الإنسانية
الحركات والمتحركات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'infinite' here refers to potential infinity (a process that never ends).
Correction: The argument targets actual infinite sets that are fully realized and exist simultaneously, not potential infinity.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude from applying one infinite series to another?
A) That two infinites can be equal
B) That the argument is decisive and unanswerable
C) That infinite numbers are possible in existence
D) That souls are finite

Key Takeaway
The argument demonstrates that actual infinite sets cannot be compared without contradiction, thereby disproving their existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If an actual infinite sequence of moments is impossible, then your life has a definite end. Are you living with the urgency that your finite time demands, or are you procrastinating as if you have an infinite future?
?
The proof shows that two infinite sets cannot be compared—what does this teach you about measuring your Lord's attributes against anything in creation? How often do you unconsciously try to size up Allah's mercy or power against human standards?
?
You believe your soul is created and finite. Does the impossibility of an actual infinite give you comfort that your existence is meaningful within a finite span? How will you invest this finite life to secure an infinite reward?

The Proof That the World Must Be Created
A kalam proof that the world must be created

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam proof that an unchanged cause cannot produce a temporal effect without a new cause, and why this proves the world must be created.

Core Idea
An unchanged agent cannot produce a new effect — therefore the world had a beginning.

This passage presents the core argument of kalam for the creation of the world. The theologian begins by examining the conditions under which a preponderant cause produces its effect, then uses pure reason to show that if the cause remains completely unchanged, it cannot produce anything new. Since the world does exist, something must have changed — leading to the conclusion that the world is created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the argument's flow.
Pay attention to the repeated structures starting with 'إما أن يقال'.
2
Identify the main logical move
Notice how the theologian uses 'pure reason' to assert that if an essence is completely unchanged, it cannot produce a new effect now if it produced none before.
This is the key step: an unchanged cause cannot give rise to a temporal effect.
3
Examine the list of possible causes
The passage lists possible new affairs that could have occurred: intention, will, nature, power, etc.
This list shows the theologian's comprehensive approach to causal explanations.
4
Understand the regress argument
The new cause itself must be either an attribute of the essence or a separate entity, and the same reasoning applies to it, leading to an infinite regress.
The regress shows that assuming an unchanged essence leads to impossibility.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The theologian concludes that the assumption of an inactive essence is false, so its opposite (that the world is created) is true.
This argument is foundational for establishing that the world had a beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي كما بالوجود احتاج إلى مرجح الجانب الوجود، والحال لا يخلو إما أن يُقال : ما يجوز أن يوجد عن المرجح يجب أن يوجد حتى لا يتراخى عنه، وإما أن يُقال: لا يجب أن يوجد حتى يتراخى عنه ، ثم يوجد بعد أن لم يوجد ، لكن العقل الصريح الذي لم يكذب يقضي أن الذات الواحدة إذا كانت من جميع جهاتها واحدة وهي كانت وكان لا يوجد عنها شيء فيما قبل مع جواز أن يوجد الآن كذلك ، فالآن لا يوجد عنها شيء وإذا كان قد وجد فقد حدث أمر (۱) لا محالة قصد عن وإرادة وطبع ،وقدرة، أو تمكن وغرض، أو سبب من الأسباب، ثم لا يخلو ذلك السبب إما (٢) أن يحدث في ذاته صفة، أو يحدث أمرًا مباينا عنه، والكلام في ذلك الحادث على أي وجه كان كالكلام في العالم، فإذا لا يجوز أن يحدث أمر ما، وإذا لم يجز فلا فرق بين حال أن يفعل وبين حال أن لا يفعل وقد وجد الفعل فهو خلف، وإنما الزمنا ) هذا لأنا وضعنا في التقدير العقلي ذاتا معطلة عن الفعل، وهو باطل فنقيضه حق
And it, as with existence needed a preponderant for the side of existence, and the case is not free from either being said: what is possible to exist from the preponderant must exist so that it is not delayed from it, or being said: it is not necessary that it exist so that it is delayed from it, then it exists after it did not exist. But pure reason that has not lied judges that the one essence when it is one from all its aspects and it was and nothing would emanate from it before, with the possibility that it emanates now as such, then now nothing emanates from it. And if it has emanated, then an affair (1) has occurred necessarily: intention and will and nature and power, or ability and purpose, or a cause among causes. Then that cause is not free from either (2) that it brings in its essence an attribute, or it brings an affair separate from it. And the discussion about that happening, whatever its manner, is like the discussion about the world. Thus it is not possible that any affair occurs, and if it is not possible then there is no difference between the state of acting and the state of not acting, and the act has occurred, so it is a contradiction. And we have forced this ( ) because we posited in mental estimation an essence inactive with respect to action, and it is false, so its opposite is true.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents a kalam proof against the eternity of the world. The theologian begins by stating the problem: If a being needs a preponderant cause to exist, then either that which is possible to exist from the cause must exist immediately (so no delay), or it need not exist immediately and can be delayed. Pure reason judges that if an essence is completely unchanged yet formerly produced nothing, then now likewise it should produce nothing. If it does produce, then something new must have occurred—intention, will, nature, power, ability, purpose, or some cause. That new cause itself must be either an attribute of the essence or a separate entity, and then the same discussion applies to that new thing, leading to an infinite regress. Thus, it is impossible for anything new to occur from an unchanged essence. But the act has occurred (the world exists), so there is a contradiction. The theologian clarifies that this impossibility arose from assuming an essence inactive with respect to action, which is a false assumption; therefore its opposite (that the world is created) is true.

Key Terms
مرجح
جواز
وجوب
ذات واحدة
عقل صريح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument implies that God cannot act because He is unchanging.
Correction: The argument does not deny God's ability; it shows that an unchanged essence cannot produce a temporal effect without a new cause. The conclusion is that the world must have been created, not that God is inactive.

Checkpoint
Why does the theologian conclude that the world must be created?
A) Because the essence changed over time
B) Because the world had a beginning in time
C) Because an unchanged essence cannot produce a new effect without a new cause
D) Because the world is contingent

Key Takeaway
An unchanged agent cannot produce a temporal effect, so the world's existence proves a new cause, which is its creation.

Three Premises and the Refutation of Eternal World
The theologian responds to opponents by listing three required premises and refuting the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three premises the theologian requires from opponents claiming the world is eternal, and the two arguments that refute the first premise concerning the possibility of the world's existence in eternity.

Core Idea
Possibility and eternity cannot coexist; if something is possible, it must have a beginning and a specifier.

In this passage, the theologian responds to the claim that the world is eternal. He lays out three premises that the opponent must prove, then immediately refutes the first premise with two powerful arguments. This establishes that the world cannot be eternal and that possibility in created things demands a creator and a temporal origin. The passage is a classic example of kalam reasoning about causality and temporality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three premises
Read the beginning of the passage and list the three premises the theologian demands from the opponent. Notice the structure: first premise about possibility in eternity, second about necessity of the possible, third about temporal causation.
These are the conditions the opponent must prove to defend the world's eternity.
2
Examine the first argument against the first premise
The theologian argues that combining the eternal (no beginning) with the temporal (has beginning) is impossible. He lists several respects in which the necessary must precede the possible.
This argument highlights the absolute distinction between the Necessary Being and contingent things.
3
Examine the second argument against the first premise
The theologian then states that an infinite chain of temporal events is impossible. This is a separate argument: the impossibility of an actual infinite regress.
This argument is based on the impossibility of actual infinity in created events.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider that if possibility enters a thing from one aspect, it enters all aspects (time, quantity, shape). Thus the world cannot be necessary or eternal; it needs a specifier.
This shows that the opponent's claim that the world is both possible and eternal is internally contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب :قلنا : أنتم مطالبون بإثبات ثلاث مقدمات : إحداها : إثبات جواز وجود العالم في الأزل. والثانية : إثبات أن ما يجوز وجوده يجب وجوده. والثالثة إثبات سبب حادث لأمر حادث. أما الأولى فنحن قد بينا استحالة وجود أمر جايز في ذاته مع وجود موجود واجب في ذاته في الأزل من وجهين : أحدهما : استحالة اقتران وجود ما لا أول له مع وجود ما له أول، فإن الجمع بينهما من المحالات، والوجود الواجب لذاته يجب أن يتقدم الوجود الجايز لذاته تقدماً ما في وجوده ؛ بحيث يكون الواجب موجودًا ولا وجود معه لا من حيث الوجود، ولا من حيث الذات أولا من حيث الزمان ولا من حيث الرتبة والمكان، ولا من حيث الفضيلة، وبالجملة فحيث ما تحقق الجواز والإمكان في شيء تحقق نفي الأزلية عنه وإثبات الأولية له واقتران الأزلية والأولية في شيء واحد محال. وقولكم : العالم جايز الوجود وكل جايز وجوده يجب وجوده أزلا وأبدًا جمع بين قضيتين متناقضتين، ومن اعترف بالجواز والإمكان فقد سلم المسألة من كل وجه، فإن الشيء إذا تطرق إليه الجواز في الوجود من وجه تطرق إليه الجواز من ساير الوجوه ؛ أعني : الوقت والمقدار والشكل، إذا كان الموجود قابلا لها أو لواحد منها، والداير بين وجوه الجواز إذا تخصص بأحد الجايزين دون الثاني احتاج إلى مخصص وانتفى الوجوب والدوام عنه. وأما الوجه الثاني في بيان الاستحالة أن الحوادث التي لا تتناهى قد تبين استحالتها. فإن قيل : ما المانع من وجود العالم في الأزل؟ كان الجواب استحالة حوادث لا تتناهى لما بينا من البرهان الدال عليه.
And the answer: We say: You are required to prove three premises: The first: proving the possibility of the existence of the world in eternity. The second: proving that what is possible to exist must exist. The third: proving a temporal cause for a temporal thing. As for the first, we have already shown the impossibility of the existence of a thing possible in itself with the existence of a self-necessary existent in eternity in two aspects: One of them: the impossibility of the conjunction of the existence of that which has no beginning with that which has a beginning, for the combination of them is among the impossibilities. And the self-necessary existence must precede the self-possible existence by some precedence in its existence, such that the necessary is existent and there is no existence with it, neither from the perspective of existence, nor from the perspective of essence, nor from the perspective of time, nor from the perspective of rank and place, nor from the perspective of virtue. In summary, wherever possibility and contingency are realized in a thing, the negation of eternity from it and the affirmation of temporality for it are realized, and the conjunction of eternity and temporality in one thing is impossible. And your statement: The world is possible of existence, and every possible of existence must exist eternally and perpetually, is a combination of two contradictory propositions. And whoever acknowledges possibility and contingency has conceded the issue from every aspect. For if possibility in existence enters into a thing from one aspect, it enters into it from all other aspects, namely: time, quantity, and shape, if the existent is receptive to them or to one of them. And that which is surrounded by possible aspects, if it becomes specified with one of the two possibles rather than the other, it needs a specifier, and necessity and perpetuity are negated from it. As for the second aspect in demonstrating impossibility: that non-terminating temporal events have been shown to be impossible. If it is said: What prevents the existence of the world in eternity? The answer is: the impossibility of non-terminating temporal events, due to the proof we have shown indicating it.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a response to an opponent who claims the world is eternal. The theologian demands three premises: (1) the possibility of the world's existence in eternity, (2) that what is possible must exist, and (3) a temporal cause for a temporal effect. He then refutes the first premise. He gives two arguments. First, it is impossible to combine the eternal (what has no beginning) with the temporal (what has a beginning). The self-necessary existent must precede the possible in existence in every respect—existence, essence, time, rank, place, and virtue. Wherever possibility is found, temporality is affirmed, so eternity and temporality cannot coexist in one thing. The opponent's claim that the world is possible yet eternal is contradictory. Moreover, if possibility enters from one aspect, it enters from all aspects, so the world would need a specifier and thus cannot be necessary or eternal. Second, the impossibility of an infinite chain of temporal events is argued: if someone asks why the world cannot be eternal, the answer is the impossibility of an actual infinite regress of events.

Key Terms
مقدمات
جواز
وجوب
أزل
أولية
مخصص
حوادث لا تتناهى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent might think that possibility alone implies existence, or that a possible thing could be eternal without a cause.
Correction: The theologian shows that possibility negates necessity and requires a specifier. If something is possible, it cannot be eternal because possibility entails temporality and dependence on a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the theologian, why is it impossible for the world to be eternal?
A) Because God created it in time
B) Because possibility and eternity are contradictory
C) Because the world is made of atoms
D) Because the philosophers argued it

Key Takeaway
The world cannot be eternal because possibility and eternity are contradictory; possibility necessitates a temporal beginning and a specifier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if the world had a beginning and is sustained by a Creator every moment, or do you sometimes slip into acting as though it just 'is' on its own? How would your gratitude change if you constantly remembered that everything exists only because a Necessary Being gives it existence at each instant?
?
The lesson says that wherever possibility is found, a specifier is needed. In your own life, do you recognize that every good thing—your health, your faith, your provision—comes from a specific Giver, or do you attribute them to chance or your own effort? What would it mean for your humility and dependence if you truly believed that nothing in your life is without a specifier?
?
If the world cannot be eternal, then your very existence is contingent on a Necessary Being who chose to bring you into being. Are you living with the urgency of one who exists only because God willed it, or are you living as if you are self-sufficient? What one change can you make today to acknowledge your complete dependence on Him?

First Proof: Impossibility of Combining Eternal and Temporal
Presents the first proof against co-eternity of the world with God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first kalam proof for the temporal origination of the world, which argues that an eternal and a temporal being cannot coexist in the same order of existence.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being must exist alone before any possible being in every respect.

This lesson introduces the first of several proofs used by kalam theologians to demonstrate that the universe is not eternal. The argument focuses on the logical impossibility of combining an eternal being (no beginning) with a temporal being (has a beginning) in the same existence. The author presents a clear case that the Necessary Being must precede all possible beings in multiple dimensions, not just time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal English translation. Let the argument’s flow sink in before examining details.
Notice the concise style – every phrase is packed with meaning.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage opens with a statement of impossibility: combining an eternal being (no beginning) with a temporal being (has a beginning) is impossible. Identify this claim.
This is the thesis that the rest of the passage unpacks.
3
Understand the five dimensions of precedence
The author lists five ways the Necessary Being must precede possible beings: existence, essence, time, rank/place, and virtue. Notice that these are not all temporal – some are ontological or hierarchical.
The phrase 'لا من حيث... ولا من حيث...' negates any co-existence in these dimensions.
4
Grasp the summary principle
The passage concludes: wherever possibility and contingency are found, eternality is negated and a beginning is affirmed. This ties the five dimensions back to the core argument.
This principle is the logical foundation of the proof.
5
Connect to the larger proof
Reflect on how this argument fits into the broader kalam project of proving the world’s temporal origination. Why does impossibility of co-eternity support that?
The proof shows that if the world is possible, it must have a beginning, and thus cannot be eternal with God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما : استحالة اقتران وجود ما لا أول له مع وجود ما له أول، فإن الجمع بينهما من المحالات، والوجود الواجب لذاته يجب أن يتقدم الوجود الجايز لذاته تقدماً ما في وجوده؛ بحيث يكون الواجب موجودًا ولا وجود معه لا من حيث الوجود، ولا من حيث الذات ولا من حيث الزمان ولا من حيث الرتبة والمكان، ولا من حيث الفضيلة، وبالجملة فحيث ما تحقق الجواز والإمكان في شيء تحقق نفي الأزلية عنه وإثبات الأولية له واقتران الأزلية والأولية في شيء واحد محال.
One of them: the impossibility of combining the existence of that which has no beginning with the existence of that which has a beginning, for combining them is among impossibilities. The existence which is necessary in itself must precede the existence which is possible in itself by some precedence in its existence, such that the Necessary exists while there is no existence with it, neither with respect to existence, nor with respect to essence, nor with respect to time, nor with respect to rank and place, nor with respect to virtue. In summary, wherever possibility and contingency are realized in a thing, the negation of eternality and affirmation of beginning are realized for it, and the conjunction of eternality and beginning in one thing is impossible.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage presents one of the proofs for the temporal origination of the world. It argues from the impossibility of combining an eternal being (no beginning) with a temporal being (has a beginning) in the same order of existence. The Necessary Being must precede all possible beings in a non-temporal sense, so they cannot be co-eternal.

أولية
جواز
وجوب
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Precedence here means temporal priority only.
Correction: The author explicitly includes non-temporal dimensions such as existence, essence, rank, and virtue, showing that precedence is ontological and hierarchical, not merely temporal.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what is necessarily negated from something that has possibility?
A) Eternality (أزلية)
B) Beginning (أولية)
C) Existence
D) Necessity

Key Takeaway
The first proof establishes that the Necessary Being must exist prior to any possible being in multiple dimensions, making co-eternity impossible.

?
You have read that the Necessary Being must exist alone before any possible being. Is there anything in your life—status, wealth, relationships—that you treat as if it has always been and will always be? What would it cost you to strip those of their false eternality and place God alone as your unshakable priority?

Refuting Necessary Emanation
The author refutes necessary emanation by logical analysis of the preponderant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of the doctrine of necessary emanation (al-ījāb al-dhātī) and why the preponderant cannot be the essence alone.

Core Idea
Necessary emanation collapses because pure essence cannot necessitate creation.

The author begins by stating his method: first refute the opponent, then indicate the true meaning of creation. He then presents a logical argument: the world is contingent and needs a preponderant. The preponderant cannot be the essence or existence as such, because that would make everything preponderate or produce infinite effects. Nor can it be a negative notion like necessary existence in itself, as that does not entail bestowing existence. This refutes necessary emanation from every angle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the author's announcement of method.
The passage is a complete argument.
2
Identify the preponderant alternatives
The author considers possible ways the preponderant could act: as essence, as existence, with a quality, with an aspect. List them.
3
Trace the refutation
Follow how each alternative is shown impossible. Note the key phrase 'فبطل الإيجاب الذاتي من كل وجه'.
4
Understand the negative notion
The author argues that 'necessary existence in itself' is negative; it does not imply bestowing existence. Reflect on why.
This is the core of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأولى أن نسلك في حل الإشكال طريق إبطال مذهب الخصم، ثم نشير إلى ما يلوح للعقل من معنى الإيجاد. فنقول : اتفقنا على أن العالم جايز لذاته، محتاج إلى مرجح الجانب (1) على العدم، فالمرجح لا يخلو إما أن يرجح من حيث هو ذات، أو من حيث هو لا وجود، ويلزم عليه أمران ؛ أحدهما أن يكون كل ذات وكل وجود مرجحا والثاني أن يحدث ما يجوز وجوده مما لا يتناهى فإن نسبة الجميع إلى الذات والوجود نسبة واجدة والوجهان محالان، وإما أن يرجح من حيث هو ذات أو وجود على صفة [أو على اعتبار ووجه، فإن يرجح من حيث هو وجود على صفة فقد سلمت المسألة وبطل الإيجاب الذاتي، وأن يرجح من حيث هو وجود على وجه ، كما قال الخصم : إنه واجب الوجود لذاته، أو إنما أوجب من حيث إنه واجب لذاته وهو وجود على وجه فهو أيضا باطل، فإن وجوب الوجود لذاته معناه أمر سلبي ؛ أي وجوده غير من غيره، ومن فهم وجودًا غير مستفاد لم يلزمه أن يفهم أنه مفيد غيره، وكذلك من فهم أنه عالم أو عقل وعاقل كما قال الخصم - لم يلزم منه أن يفهم منه أنه موجد مفيد الوجود غيره ؛ لأن معنى كونه عقلا عند الخصم معنى سلبي أيضاً ؛ أي مجرد عن المادة، وبأن يكون مجردًا عن المادة لا يلزم أن يكون مفيد الوجود لغيره، فبطل الإيجاب الذاتي من كل وجه وتعين أنه إنما أوجد لكونه على صفة ولتلك الصفة من حيث ذاتها صلاحية التخصيص والإيجاد عموما بالنسبة إلى ما حصل على الهيئة التي حصل، وبالنسبة إلى غيرها نسبة واحدة، ولها أيضا خصوص وجه بالنسبة إلى ما حصل دون ما لم يحصل، وذلك الخصوص لها عند إضافتها إياها إلى غيرها. فنقول : لما علم الباري وجود العالم في الوقت الذي وُجد فيه أراد وجوده في ذلك الوقت، فالعلم عام التعلق ؛ بمعنى أنه صفة صالحة لأن يعلم به جميع ما يصح أن يعلم والمعلومات لا تتناهى على معنى أنه علم وجود العالم وعلم جواز وجوده قبل وبعد على كل وجه يتطرق إليه الجواز العقلي والإرادة عامة التعلق ؛ بمعنى أنها صفة صالحة لتخصيص ما يجوز أن يخصص به ، والمرادات لا تتناهى على معنى أن وجوه الجواز في التخصيصات غير متناهية، ولها خصوص تعلق من حيث (٤) (٥)
And it is better that we follow, in solving the problem, the way of refuting the opponent's doctrine, then point to what appears to the intellect of the meaning of bringing into existence (ījād). We say: We agree that the world is contingent in itself (jā'iz li-dhātihī), in need of a preponderant (murajjiḥ) to favor the side of existence over non-existence. The preponderant must either preponderate in virtue of being an essence (dhāt), or in virtue of being an existence (wujūd). Two things would follow: first, that every essence and every existence would be preponderant; second, that everything possible would come to existence ad infinitum, because the relation of all to the essence and existence is one relation. Both are impossible. Or it preponderates in virtue of being an essence or existence with a quality (ṣifa) or with a consideration and aspect. If it preponderates in virtue of being an existence with a quality, then the question is settled and necessary emanation (al-ījāb al-dhātī) is invalidated. And that it preponderates in virtue of being an existence in an aspect, as the opponent said: He is necessarily existent in Himself (wājib al-wujūd li-dhātihī), or He only causes because He is necessary in Himself and He is an existence in an aspect—this too is false. For necessary existence in itself (wujūb al-wujūd li-dhātihī) is a negative notion: meaning that His existence is not derived from another. One who understands an existence not derived does not necessarily understand that it is bestowing existence to another. Likewise, one who understands that He is Knowing, or Intellect and Intelligent as the opponent said—it does not necessarily follow from that that He is a bringer-into-existence, bestowing existence to another; because the meaning of His being Intellect according to the opponent is also negative: i.e., abstracted from matter. By being abstracted from matter, it does not necessitate that He is bestowing existence to another. Thus necessary emanation is invalidated from every aspect, and it is determined that He only brings into existence because of a quality (ṣifa). And that quality, in virtue of its essence, has the suitability (ṣalāḥiyya) for specification and bringing into existence generally with respect to what came to be in the form it came to be, and with respect to other things it is one relation. It also has a specific aspect (khuṣūṣ wajh) with respect to what came to be, not what did not come to be. And that specificity belongs to it when it is related to other things. We say: When the Eternal knew the existence of the world at the time it was brought into existence, He willed its existence at that time. Knowledge is general in attachment: meaning it is a quality suitable for knowing all that can be known, and the objects of knowledge are infinite in the sense that He knew the existence of the world and knew the possibility of its existence before and after in every aspect to which rational possibility extends. Will is general in attachment: meaning it is a quality suitable for specifying what can be specified, and the objects of will are infinite in the sense that the aspects of possibility in specifications are infinite. And they have a specific attachment in terms of (text ends).
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a combined refutation and constructive argument. The author announces his method: first refute the opponent's theory (necessary emanation), then indicate what reason perceives as the true meaning of creation. He then presents a logical argument: the world is contingent and needs a preponderant. The preponderant cannot be the essence alone (either as absolute essence or as absolute existence) because that would imply everything would preponderate or everything possible would exist infinitely. Nor can it be the essence considered as having a specific aspect like 'necessary existence in itself' or 'intellect', because these are negative notions that do not entail bestowing existence. Thus necessary emanation is refuted. The correct view is that God creates because of a quality (ṣifa). This quality has universal suitability for specification (general aspect) but also a specific aspect that determines this particular existent. He then applies this to knowledge and will: they are general in attachment (can relate to all objects) but also have a specific attachment (by virtue of the aspect) that distinguishes the actual from the possible. The text ends mid-sentence on page 39, presumably continuing to explain that specific attachment.

Key Terms
المرجح
الإيجاب الذاتي
وجوب الوجود لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's necessary existence means He necessarily creates the world.
Correction: Necessary existence in itself is a negative notion; it does not entail bestowing existence. Creation requires a quality (ṣifa) of specification.

Checkpoint
Why did the author reject the idea that the preponderant is the essence considered as 'necessary existence in itself'?
A) Because necessary existence is a positive attribute that cannot be shared.
B) Because necessary existence in itself is a negative notion that does not imply bestowing existence.
C) Because the essence cannot be a preponderant at all.
D) Because the opponent did not actually hold that view.

Key Takeaway
Necessary emanation is refuted because pure essence or negative attributes cannot necessitate creation.

Creation through a Quality: Universal and Specific Aspects
The author explains the true meaning of creation through a quality with universal and specific aspects, applied to knowledge and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God creates through a quality (ṣifa) that has both universal suitability and specific aspect, as exemplified by knowledge and will.

Core Idea
Creation is not from essence alone but from a quality with universal and specific aspects.

Having refuted necessary emanation, the author now presents the positive account. He argues that God brings into existence only because of a quality. This quality, in its essence, has suitability (ṣalāḥiyya) for specification and creation in general, but also has a specific aspect that determines this particular existent. He then applies this to divine knowledge and will: they are general in attachment (can relate to all objects) but also have a specific attachment (by virtue of the aspect) that distinguishes the actual from the possible. The text ends mid-sentence, indicating further elaboration.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage that begins with 'وتعين أنه إنما أوجد لكونه على صفة'. Notice the progression from refutation to positive view.
The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating it continues.
2
Understand the role of the quality
The author says God creates because of a quality. This quality has 'صلاحية التخصيص والإيجاد عموما' (suitability for specification and creation in general). Ask: what does this mean?
3
Distinguish universal and specific aspects
The quality has a universal aspect (general suitability) and a specific aspect (خصوص وجه) that determines this particular creation. Identify these in the text.
4
Apply to knowledge and will
The author says knowledge and will are general in attachment (عام التعلق) but also have specific attachment. Reflect on how this explains God's particular choice.
This is the start of a longer argument that continues on the next page.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's knowledge and will have universal scope, yet He specifically brought you into existence at this time and place. How does this realization change your view of your own purpose and accountability before Him? What will you do today to live in light of His specific will for you?

The passage highlights that God's attributes have both general suitability and specific application. Are you living as if God's general mercy is enough, or are you seeking His specific guidance for your life? What is one concrete step you can take to submit to His specific will?
?
If God's will is general in attachment but specifically wills what happens, then nothing in your life is accidental. What area of your life do you still treat as random or outside God's control? How can you surrender that area to His specific will today?

Answer to the Objection that God is Cause of Himself
Author answers the objection that God being a cause of Himself is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that God cannot be a cause of Himself by clarifying the meaning of causality and the two types of simultaneity.

Core Idea
God is the source of all existence without temporal or essential simultaneity.

The objection that God cannot be a cause of Himself relies on a misunderstanding of causality. In this passage, the author explains that causality does not require simultaneity; rather, God's precedence is logical and ontological. We will explore the key distinctions that resolve the doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice how the author addresses the third doubt.
Pay attention to the terms 'معية بالذات' and 'معية بالزمان'.
2
Identify the two types of simultaneity
The passage rejects both essential simultaneity and temporal simultaneity between cause and effect. Understand why each is impossible for God.
Essential simultaneity would negate causality; temporal simultaneity would make God temporal.
3
Understand God's ontological precedence
The conclusion is that God must precede the world in existence because His existence is self-sufficient, while the world's existence is derived from Him.
This precedence is not temporal but logical and ontological.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك الجواب عن الشبهة الثالثة أنه علة لذاته، فإن معنى كونه علة أي مبدئ لوجود شيء، وإنما الممتنع وجود المعلول مع العلة معية بالذات، فإن ذلك يرفع العلية والمعلولية، وكذلك نقول: يستحيل وجود المعلول مع العلة معية بالزمان، فإن ذلك يوجب كون العلة زمانية قابلة للتغير، وذلك محال أيضا، فيجب أن يتقدم في الوجود؛ لأن وجود الباري سبحانه وجود لذاته غير مستفاد من غيره، ووجود العالم مستفاد منه، والمفيد أبدا يتقدم في الوجود.
And likewise the answer to the third doubt: that He is a cause of Himself. For the meaning of His being a cause is that He is a source for the existence of something. And it is only impossible for the effect to exist with the cause in essential simultaneity, for that would negate causality and effecthood. And also we say: It is impossible for the effect to exist with the cause in temporal simultaneity, for that would necessitate the cause being temporal and subject to change, and that is also impossible. Thus the cause must precede in existence, because the existence of the Exalted Creator is an existence by itself, not derived from another, while the existence of the world is derived from Him, and the bestower always precedes in existence.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is the answer to a third objection: that God cannot be a cause of Himself because a cause must precede its effect. The author clarifies that God's being a 'cause' (علة) means He is the source (مبدئ) of existence. The objection conflates two types of simultaneity: essential simultaneity (معية بالذات) and temporal simultaneity (معية بالزمان). Essential simultaneity would imply the cause and effect are co-existent in essence, which would negate causality. Temporal simultaneity would make God temporal and subject to change. The correct view is that God precedes the world in existence because His existence is self-sufficient (غير مستفاد من غيره) while the world's existence is derived from Him. Thus, the bestower must precede the recipient.

Key Terms
علة
مبدئ
معلول
معية بالذات
معية بالزمان
مستفاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Causation requires temporal simultaneity between cause and effect.
Correction: The author shows that temporal simultaneity would make the cause temporal and subject to change, which is impossible for God. Instead, God's precedence is ontological, not temporal.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the effect cannot exist with the cause in temporal simultaneity?
A) Because it would make the cause temporal and subject to change.
B) Because it would make the cause impossible.
C) Because it would deny the existence of the world.
D) Because it would imply the cause is derived from another.

Key Takeaway
God's existence is self-sufficient and precedes all created existence, not in time but in ontological priority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say 'God is the source of my existence' in du'as, but do your daily actions reflect a deep, moment-by-moment awareness that your very being and every ability are derived from Him? If you truly knew that your existence is 'not derived from another', how would your reliance on people or plans shift?
?
The passage insists that God must precede all existence — not in time, but in existential priority. In what part of your life are you acting as if something else (career, family, reputation) has precedence over your relationship with the Creator? Will you give God that precedence in your choices today?
?
The impossibility of temporal simultaneity for God means He is free from change. Where in your heart do you secretly wish God would change His commands or your circumstances to suit you? How can you instead rest in His unchanging nature as the sole source of all good?

Proving the Temporality of the World through Human Souls
Establishing a method to prove the world's temporality via finite souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the stepwise argument from the finitude of human souls to the temporality of the entire world, and the principle of actual infinity that supports it.

Core Idea
The finitude of human souls leads stepwise to the temporality of the whole cosmos.

In this passage, the author presents a clever argument for the temporality of the world, starting from something we can observe: human souls. By establishing that human souls are finite in number, and then tracing the implications through persons, mixtures, circular motions, and celestial bodies, he concludes that the entire world must be temporally originated. This method, he claims, is the easiest and best. Read on to see the chain of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own structure come through.
Notice that the passage describes a chain of dependencies.
2
Identify the chain of reasoning
The author lists five steps: souls → persons → mixtures → circular motions → celestial bodies. Trace how each step leads to the next.
This is a descending causal chain from souls to the whole world.
3
Understand the principle about actual infinity
The author states a key principle: an actual infinite cannot be increased or decreased. This justifies why each step must be finite.
This principle prevents infinite regress.
4
Reflect on why this method is easiest
The author claims this is the easiest and best method. Consider why: it starts from something accessible (human souls) and uses a straightforward chain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يعتمد في إثبات حدث العالم أن نفرض الكلام في النفوس الإنسانية ونثبت تناهيها عددًا، ثم ترتب عليها تناهي الأشخاص الإنسانية، وترتب عليها حدوث الأمزجة وتناهيها، ثم نرتب عليها تناهي الحركات الدورية الجامعة للعناصر، ثم ترتب عليها تناهي المتحركات والمحركات السماوية، فثبت حدث العالم بأسره، وهي أسهل الطرق وأحسنها. فنقول: قد تقرر في أوائل العقول أن كل عدد أو معدود موجود بالفعل إذا كان غير متناه بالفعل، فإنه لا يقبل الزيادة والنقصان، وإن الزايد لا يكون مثل الناقص.
Among the things relied upon in proving the temporality of the world is that we posit discourse concerning human souls and we establish their finitude in number, then we order upon them the finitude of human persons, and we order upon them the occurrence of mixtures and their finitude, then we order upon them the finitude of the circular motions that encompass the elements, then we order upon them the finitude of the celestial moving bodies and movers; thus the temporality of the entire world is established, and this is the easiest and best of the methods. So we say: It has been established at the beginnings of intellects that every number or counted thing existing actually, if it is actually infinite, does not admit increase or decrease, and that the increased is not like the decreased.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents a method for proving the temporality (ḥudūth) of the world. The author proposes a stepwise argument: first, we consider human souls (al-nufūs al-insāniyya) and prove that they are finite in number. Since each human person has a soul, the finitude of souls implies the finitude of human persons. Then, from the finitude of human persons, we infer the finitude (and thus occurrence) of the mixtures (al-amzija) that constitute their bodies. Next, from the finitude of mixtures (which are composed of the four elements), we deduce the finitude of the circular motions (al-ḥarakāt al-dawriyya) that encompass the elements—i.e., the celestial sphere motions. Finally, from the finitude of these motions, we conclude the finitude of the celestial bodies and their movers. Thus the entire world is shown to be temporally originated (ḥādith). The author claims this is the easiest and best method. He then adds a principle: any actual infinite number or counted thing cannot admit increase or decrease; if it did, the increased would not be equal to the decreased. This principle underlies the proof that the numbers in each step must be finite.

Key Terms
حدث العالم
نفوس إنسانية
تناهي
أمزجة
حركات دورية
علوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an infinite series can be added to without contradiction.
Correction: The author asserts that an actual infinite cannot be increased or decreased; if it could, the increased would not equal the decreased, which is contradictory.

Checkpoint
What is the first step in the author's method to prove the temporality of the world?
A) Proving the finitude of celestial motions
B) Proving the finitude of human souls
C) Proving the finitude of human persons
D) Proving the finitude of mixtures

Key Takeaway
The temporality of the world can be proven by a chain of reasoning that begins with the finitude of human souls and ends with the finitude of celestial motions, supported by the principle that an actual infinite cannot be increased or decreased.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof begins with your own soul. Do you truly believe that your soul is finite and had a beginning? If so, how does that belief shape the way you invest your time and energy today? What changes if you truly lived as if your soul had a start date?
?
If the chain of reasoning in this passage is correct, then the entire universe is contingent and originated. What does that imply about the One who brought it into existence? Does your daily life reflect that you are living in a world that depends entirely on its Creator every moment?
?
The author claims this is the easiest method. When you read it, did you find it convincing? If not, what resistance arose in your heart? Could it be that you prefer to think the world is eternal because it lets you avoid the implications of having a Creator who holds you accountable?

The First Cause: Potential as Power, Not Capacity
Refutation of the claim that the First Cause was potential and solving the third doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between 'potential' as capacity vs. power in the context of the First Cause, and how the author refutes the claim that a cause without change must be a cause by essence.

Core Idea
The First Cause is powerful, not merely capable.

This passage tackles a key objection against attributing actual creation to God. The opponents argue that if God was 'potentially' a maker, that implies imperfection. The author clarifies that 'potential' is ambiguous, and the relevant sense is power, not passive capacity. He then resolves a related doubt about causality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of 'potential'
Notice that the author begins by analyzing the term 'potential' (قوة). He distinguishes between pure capacity (استعداد مجرد) and power (قدرة). Read the Arabic carefully to see how he uses this distinction.
The first meaning (pure capacity) implies imperfection and is not conceded.
2
Understand which meaning is conceded
The author concedes that the First Cause has 'potential' in the sense of power, but not in the sense of passive capacity. This is the point of dispute.
He also says there is no need for an 'exit into actuality' because power is already actual.
3
Read the solution to the third doubt
The third doubt claims that any cause without change is a cause by its essence. The author refutes this by citing the First Intellect, which does not change but is not a cause by essence.
The First Intellect is an effect of the Necessary Existent and is a cause only by virtue of being necessary through Him.
4
Apply the reasoning to separate intellects
The author generalizes: separate intellects (عقول مفارقة) also do not change and are not causes by their essences. This strengthens his refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : كان صانعا بالقوة أيضاً مشترك، فإن القوة يراد بها الاستعداد المجرد ويراد بها القدرة، والأول غير مسلّم، والثاني مسلّم، وهو محل النزاع ولا يحتاج إلى مخرج إلى الفعل. وأما حل الشبهة الثالثة ، قال : كل علة لا يجوز عليها التحرك والاستحالة، فإنما تكون علة من جهة ذاتها غير مسلّم، بل هو باطل على أصله بالعقل الأول، فإنه لا يجوز عليه التحرك والاستحالة، وليس علة من جهة ذاته، بل هو معلول الواجب الوجود وعلة من جهة كونه واجبًا به لا بذاته وكذلك العقول المفارقة لا تقبل الحركة والاستحالة وليست علل من جهة ذواتها.
And their statement: 'He was a maker potentially' is equivocal, for 'potential' is used to mean pure capacity and is used to mean power. The first is not conceded, and the second is conceded, and it is the point of dispute and does not need an exit into actuality. As for solving the third doubt, he said: Every cause which does not admit change and transformation is a cause by virtue of its essence—this is not conceded; rather it is false according to his principle concerning the First Intellect, for it does not admit change and transformation, and it is not a cause by virtue of its essence, but rather it is an effect of the Necessary Existent and a cause by virtue of its being necessary through Him not by itself, and likewise the separate intellects do not accept motion and transformation and are not causes by virtue of their essences.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection and a resolution of the third doubt. The author begins by analyzing the phrase 'He was a maker potentially.' He argues that the term 'potential' (al-quwwa) is ambiguous: it can refer to pure capacity (al-istiʿdād al-mujarrad) or to power (al-qudra). The first meaning (pure capacity) is not conceded regarding the First Cause, as it would imply imperfection, while the second meaning (power) is conceded, and that is the point at issue. No need to posit an exit from potentiality to actuality. Then the author addresses the third doubt, which asserts that any cause that does not admit change or transformation is a cause by virtue of its essence. The author denies this, using the First Intellect (al-ʿaql al-awwal) as an example: the First Intellect does not admit change or transformation, yet it is not a cause by virtue of its essence; rather, it is an effect of the Necessary Existent (wājib al-wujūd) and is a cause only by virtue of its being necessary through Him, not by itself. The same applies to the separate intellects: they are free from motion and transformation and are not causes by virtue of their essences.

Key Terms
قوة
استعداد مجرد
قدرة
علة
واجب الوجود
عقل أول
عقول مفارقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Potential always implies imperfection and passivity.
Correction: The author shows that 'potential' can also mean power, which is active and does not imply imperfection. The First Cause is powerful, not merely capable.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which meaning of 'potential' (قوة) is conceded regarding the First Cause?
A) Pure capacity (استعداد مجرد)
B) Power (قدرة)
C) Both meanings equally
D) Neither meaning

Key Takeaway
The First Cause is powerful by essence, not merely capable; and being a cause without change does not make something a cause by essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where you feel God's help is merely 'potential' but not yet actual, how does this lesson challenge you to instead believe in His active power (qudra) right now? What one situation will you consciously remind yourself that He is powerful, not merely capable, starting today?
?
The third doubt assumes that if something doesn't change, it must be a cause by itself. Have you ever thought that because God seems unchanging or distant, He is not actively causing your circumstances? How does the refutation using the First Intellect (immutable yet caused) correct this? Will you confess any such assumption to God today?

The Proof Structure and the Principle of the Infinite
The author presents the proof's structure and the core principle about infinite collections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the overall chain of proof for the temporal origination of the world from human souls and the key principle that an actually infinite collection cannot admit increase or decrease.

Core Idea
An actual infinite cannot change, but human souls increase—so they must be finite.

The author introduces a powerful argument for the temporality of the world based on human souls. He first outlines a chain of reasoning from the finitude of souls to the finitude of all motion. Then he lays down a fundamental principle: any actually infinite collection cannot admit increase or decrease. This principle is the logical heart of the proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the chain
Read the Arabic passage once to see the overall structure: first the chain of dependencies, then the principle. Notice how each step leads to the next.
The chain starts with souls and ends with the whole world.
2
Understand the principle about infinity
Focus on the principle: an actual infinite cannot be increased or decreased. Ask yourself why that must be true. The author gives several reasons.
This principle is crucial for the rest of the argument.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the principle matters. If something can be increased, it cannot be actually infinite. This will apply to souls in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يعتمد في إثبات حدث العالم أن نفرض الكلام في النفوس الإنسانية ونثبت تناهيها عددًا، ثم ترتب عليها تناهي الأشخاص الإنسانية، وترتب عليها حدوث الأمزجة وتناهيها، ثم نرتب عليها تناهي الحركات الدورية الجامعة للعناصر، ثم ترتب عليها تناهي المتحركات والمحركات السماوية، فثبت حدث العالم بأسره، وهي أسهل الطرق وأحسنها. فنقول : قد تقرر في أوائل العقول أن كل عدد أو معدود موجود بالفعل إذا كان غير متناه بالفعل، فإنه لا يقبل الزيادة والنقصان، وإن الزايد لا يكون مثل الناقص قط، ولا يمكن أن يكون شيء أكبر من مقدار غير متناه لموجود وجد، وغير المتناهي لا يتضاعف بما لا يتناهى، وإن ما يتناهى من طرف يتناهى من سائر الأطراف، والنفوس الإنسانية عند القوم موجودات بالفعل مجتمعة في الوجود، قابلة للزيادة والنقصان فإن الزمان الذي نحن فيه قد اشتمل على أشخاص إنسانية متناهية ولكل شخص نفس، إذا أضفت تلك النفوس إلى ما مضى من الأشخاص التي بقيت نفوسها كانت الأوائل منها أنقص، وهي مع الزيادة أزيد، وكذلك في كل زمان وساعة تتزايد النفوس أبدًا، وما قبل الزيادة في كل وقت يستحيل أن يكون غير متناه ؛ وذلك لأن نسبة ما مضى إلى الحاصل كنسبة الناقص إلى الزايد، وحيث ما تمادى إلى الأول صار أنقص، كما إذا تمادى إلى الآخر صار أزيد ، ومثل هذا مستحيل فيما لا يتناهى موجودًا بالفعل محصوراً في الوجود.
And among what is relied upon in proving the temporal origination of the world is that we posit discourse concerning human souls and establish their finitude in number, then we order upon it the finitude of human persons, and order upon it the occurrence of temperaments and their finitude, then we order upon it the finitude of the encompassing circular motions of the elements, then we order upon it the finitude of the celestial movers and moved things; thus the temporal origination of the entire world is established. This is the easiest and best of ways. So we say: It has been established in the first principles of reason that every number or numbered thing actually existent, if it is actually infinite, then it does not admit increase and decrease, and the increased is never like the decreased, nor can anything be greater than an infinite magnitude for an existent that exists, and the infinite is not multiplied by what is infinite, and whatever is finite from one side is finite from all sides. And human souls, according to the [philosophers], are actually existent things gathered together in existence, admitting increase and decrease. For the time in which we are has encompassed a finite number of human persons, and each person has a soul. If you add those souls to the past persons whose souls have remained, the earlier ones among them are less, and they are more with increase, and likewise in every time and hour the souls increase continuously. And what is before increase at every time is impossible to be infinite. That is because the ratio of what has passed to what is obtained is like the ratio of the decreased to the increased; just as its progression to the first makes it less, so its progression to the last makes it more. And such is impossible for that which is actually infinite and contained in existence.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the world based on the finitude of human souls. The author outlines a chain of reasoning: first, establish that human souls are finite in number; then, this implies the finitude of human persons; then, the occurrence and finitude of temperaments (al-ammiza); then, the finitude of the encompassing circular motions of the elements; and finally, the finitude of celestial movers and moved things, thus proving that the entire world came into being. The author notes this is the easiest and best method. The proof then delves into the principle that an actually infinite collection cannot admit increase or decrease, and that an increased infinite is impossible. Human souls are actual existents that increase over time (e.g., past souls are fewer than present ones). Since they admit increase, they cannot be actually infinite; therefore, the number of souls is finite, and by the chain, the world is temporally originated.

Key Terms
حدث العالم
تناهي
عدد
غير متناه
زيادة
نقصان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an infinite series of past events is possible.
Correction: The author argues that an actual infinite (simultaneously existent) cannot admit increase, so a series that grows over time cannot be actually infinite.

Checkpoint
What is the first step in the author's chain for proving the temporal origination of the world?
A) Proving the finitude of celestial motions
B) Proving the finitude of human souls
C) Proving the occurrence of temperaments
D) Proving the finitude of human persons

Key Takeaway
The proof relies on the impossibility of an actual infinite collection that admits increase.

?

?

?

Applying the Principle to Human Souls
The author applies the principle to human souls to prove their finitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of the actual infinite is applied to human souls to prove their finitude and thus the temporal origination of the world.

Core Idea
Since souls increase over time, they cannot be actually infinite, so the world had a beginning.

Having established the principle that an actual infinite cannot admit increase or decrease, the author now applies it to human souls. He argues that souls are actual existents that increase with time, so they must be finite. This finitude then triggers the chain that leads to the temporality of the whole world.

Guided Path
1
Note that souls are actual existents that increase
The author states that human souls are actually existing and gathered together in existence. They admit increase and decrease because as time goes on, more souls come into being.
This is a key premise: souls are not just abstract numbers; they are real entities that can be counted.
2
Observe the increase over time
Compare past souls to present souls: the earlier ones are fewer, and the later ones are more. In every moment, souls increase. If souls were actually infinite, they could not increase, but they do.
Think of the total number of souls now vs. in the past: the present number is greater.
3
Conclude that souls are finite
Since souls admit increase, they cannot be actually infinite. Therefore, the number of souls is finite. By the chain from the previous lesson, this implies the world is temporally originated.
This completes the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يعتمد في إثبات حدث العالم أن نفرض الكلام في النفوس الإنسانية ونثبت تناهيها عددًا، ثم ترتب عليها تناهي الأشخاص الإنسانية، وترتب عليها حدوث الأمزجة وتناهيها، ثم نرتب عليها تناهي الحركات الدورية الجامعة للعناصر، ثم ترتب عليها تناهي المتحركات والمحركات السماوية، فثبت حدث العالم بأسره، وهي أسهل الطرق وأحسنها. فنقول : قد تقرر في أوائل العقول أن كل عدد أو معدود موجود بالفعل إذا كان غير متناه بالفعل، فإنه لا يقبل الزيادة والنقصان، وإن الزايد لا يكون مثل الناقص قط، ولا يمكن أن يكون شيء أكبر من مقدار غير متناه لموجود وجد، وغير المتناهي لا يتضاعف بما لا يتناهى، وإن ما يتناهى من طرف يتناهى من سائر الأطراف، والنفوس الإنسانية عند القوم موجودات بالفعل مجتمعة في الوجود، قابلة للزيادة والنقصان فإن الزمان الذي نحن فيه قد اشتمل على أشخاص إنسانية متناهية ولكل شخص نفس، إذا أضفت تلك النفوس إلى ما مضى من الأشخاص التي بقيت نفوسها كانت الأوائل منها أنقص، وهي مع الزيادة أزيد، وكذلك في كل زمان وساعة تتزايد النفوس أبدًا، وما قبل الزيادة في كل وقت يستحيل أن يكون غير متناه ؛ وذلك لأن نسبة ما مضى إلى الحاصل كنسبة الناقص إلى الزايد، وحيث ما تمادى إلى الأول صار أنقص، كما إذا تمادى إلى الآخر صار أزيد ، ومثل هذا مستحيل فيما لا يتناهى موجودًا بالفعل محصوراً في الوجود.
And among what is relied upon in proving the temporal origination of the world is that we posit discourse concerning human souls and establish their finitude in number, then we order upon it the finitude of human persons, and order upon it the occurrence of temperaments and their finitude, then we order upon it the finitude of the encompassing circular motions of the elements, then we order upon it the finitude of the celestial movers and moved things; thus the temporal origination of the entire world is established. This is the easiest and best of ways. So we say: It has been established in the first principles of reason that every number or numbered thing actually existent, if it is actually infinite, then it does not admit increase and decrease, and the increased is never like the decreased, nor can anything be greater than an infinite magnitude for an existent that exists, and the infinite is not multiplied by what is infinite, and whatever is finite from one side is finite from all sides. And human souls, according to the [philosophers], are actually existent things gathered together in existence, admitting increase and decrease. For the time in which we are has encompassed a finite number of human persons, and each person has a soul. If you add those souls to the past persons whose souls have remained, the earlier ones among them are less, and they are more with increase, and likewise in every time and hour the souls increase continuously. And what is before increase at every time is impossible to be infinite. That is because the ratio of what has passed to what is obtained is like the ratio of the decreased to the increased; just as its progression to the first makes it less, so its progression to the last makes it more. And such is impossible for that which is actually infinite and contained in existence.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the world based on the finitude of human souls. The author outlines a chain of reasoning: first, establish that human souls are finite in number; then, this implies the finitude of human persons; then, the occurrence and finitude of temperaments (al-ammiza); then, the finitude of the encompassing circular motions of the elements; and finally, the finitude of celestial movers and moved things, thus proving that the entire world came into being. The author notes this is the easiest and best method. The proof then delves into the principle that an actually infinite collection cannot admit increase or decrease, and that an increased infinite is impossible. Human souls are actual existents that increase over time (e.g., past souls are fewer than present ones). Since they admit increase, they cannot be actually infinite; therefore, the number of souls is finite, and by the chain, the world is temporally originated.

Key Terms

زيادة
نقصان
غير متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that souls could be infinite because they are immaterial and not subject to physical counting.
Correction: The author points out that souls are actual existents that increase in number over time, so they are not infinite. Immateriality does not prevent them from being counted as a collection.

Checkpoint
What is the ratio of past souls to present souls according to the author?
A) Like the ratio of the increased to the decreased
B) Like the ratio of the decreased to the increased
C) Like the ratio of the finite to the infinite
D) Like the ratio of the infinite to the finite

Key Takeaway
The increase of human souls over time proves they are finite, leading to the world's temporal origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If human souls are finite and the world had a beginning, then your own soul is not eternal—it was created. How does that realization affect the way you value your limited time in this world? What will you do today to prepare for the eternity that awaits after this finite life?
?
The argument shows that the number of souls increases over time. Each new soul is a new creation. If God creates every soul, then every human life has purpose. Does your life reflect that purpose? What one change can you make to live with greater intention?
?
If the world had a beginning, then it will also have an end. The author's reasoning leads to the conclusion that the universe is contingent. How does knowing that your existence is contingent on God's will change your sense of dependency on Him? In what area of your life are you acting as if you are independent?
